The anticipating brain is not a scientist: the free-energy principle from an ecological-enactive perspective
Synthese
Author(s)
Jelle Bruineberg, Julian Kiverstein, Erik Rietveld
Title
The anticipating brain is not a scientist: the free-energy principle from an ecological-enactive perspective
In

Abstract
In this paper, we argue for a theoretical separation of the free-energy principle from Helmholtzian accounts of the predictive brain. The free-energy principle is a theoretical framework capturing the imperative for biological self-organization in information-theoretic terms. The free-energy principle has typically been connected with a Bayesian theory of predictive coding, and the latter is often taken to support a Helmholtzian theory of perception as unconscious inference. If our interpretation is right, however, a Helmholtzian view of perception is incompatible with Bayesian predictive coding under the free-energy principle. We argue that the free energy principle and the ecological and enactive approach to mind and life make for a much happier marriage of ideas. We make our argument based on three points. First we argue that the free energy principle applies to the whole animalâenvironment system, and not only to the brain. Second, we show that active inference, as understood by the free-energy principle, is incompatible with unconscious inference understood as analagous to scientific hypothesis-testing, the main tenet of a Helmholtzian view of perception. Third, we argue that the notion of inference at work in Bayesian predictive coding under the free-energy principle is too weak to support a Helmholtzian theory of perception. Taken together these points imply that the free energy principle is best understood in ecological and enactive terms set out in this paper.

The models' development often overlooks real-world consequences of how they enable the extraction, commodification, and instrumentalization of meaning.The paper concludes by emphasizing the strong connection between the interpretation of meaning and the shift in power dynamics, identifying this as a critical issue for the emerging field of Critical AI Studies. This field is dedicated to scrutinizing the impact and implications of AI technologies on society, culture, and individuals.1 / 11. Introduction

h elastfewyearshaveseenamajorevolutioninnaturallanguageprocessing.

They ponder over the meaning and ramifications of the epistemological conception facilitated by the statistical regressions and data architectures in AI. They emphasize how such AI models impact the mediation and the recipient of language in these cybernetic and connectionist machines. To answer these questions, they draw upon the hermeneutics of Paul Ricoeur, focusing on his notion of the world as a referent of semantics and the text's demanding essence. This approach allows them to critique the way AI models are often perceived as "shockingly good, and completely mindless".The authors argue that Ricoeur's concept of the world can help to rethink the relationship between meaning and reflexivity. This reflexivity is seen not just as a reader's reflexivity, but also as a rediscovered reflexivity of the real world within society, culture, and political economy.In the third section, they strive to develop a sociological and critical understanding of these AI models (BERT and GPT-3). They point out the increasing intrusion of these models into everyday life and highlight that their value is inseparable from a data and meaning extraction market, where ethics can be used as a form of justification and commodity.In the brief history provided, the authors note a major evolution in natural language processing over the past few years. For the first time, language models based on Transformer architecture can generate coherent texts without relying on previously decided symbolic and deductive logic. This type of programming, which imitates cognitive brain mechanisms using artificial neurons, gained prominence after the 2012 ImageNet competition was won by Geoffrey Hintonâs team. This success highlighted the effectiveness of using high computational power, vast data sets, and a connectionist approach.1 / 1SummariZe: Sparse Bayesian Information Filters for Localization and 
Mapping 
by 
Matthew R. Walter 
Submitted to the Joint Program in Applied Ocean Science & Engineering 
on February 6, 2008, in partial fulf i llment of the 
requirements for the degree of 
Doctor of Philosophy 
Abstract 
This thesis formulates an estimation framework for Simultaneous Localization and 
Mapping (SLAM) that addresses the problem of scalability in large environments. 
We describe an estimation-theoretic algorithm that achieves signif i cant gains in com- 
putational ef i ciency while maintaining consistent estimates for the vehicle pose and 
the map of the environment. 
We specif i cally address the feature-based SLAM problem in which the robot rep- 
resents the environment as a collection of landmarks. The thesis takes a Bayesian 
approach whereby we maintain a joint posterior over the vehicle pose and feature 
states, conditioned upon measurement data. We model the distribution as Gaus- 
sian and parametrize the posterior in the canonical form, in terms of the information 
(inverse covariance) matrix. When sparse, this representation is amenable to compu- 
tationally ef i cient Bayesian SLAM i ltering. However, while a large majority of the 
elements within the normalized information matrix are very small in magnitude, it is 
fully populated nonetheless. Recent feature-based SLAM i lters achieve the scalability 
benef i ts of a sparse parametrization by explicitly pruning these weak links in an ef f ort 
to enforce sparsity. We analyze one such algorithm, the Sparse Extended Information 
Filter (SEIF), which has laid much of the groundwork concerning the computational 
benef i ts of the sparse canonical form. The thesis performs a detailed analysis of the 
process by which the SEIF approximates the sparsity of the information matrix and 
reveals key insights into the consequences of dif f erent sparsif i cation strategies. We 
demonstrate that the SEIF yields a sparse approximation to the posterior that is in- 
consistent, suf f ering from exaggerated conf i dence estimates. This overconf i dence has 
detrimental ef f ects on important aspects of the SLAM process and af f ects the higher 
level goal of producing accurate maps for subsequent localization and path planning. 
This thesis proposes an alternative scalable i lter that maintains sparsity while 
preserving the consistency of the distribution. We leverage insights into the natural 
structure of the feature-based canonical parametrization and derive a method that 
actively maintains an exactly sparse posterior. Our algorithm exploits the structure 
of the parametrization to achieve gains in ef i ciency, with a computational cost that 
scales linearly with the size of the map.Unlike similar techniques that sacrif i ce 
consistency for improved scalability, our algorithm performs inference over a posterior 
that is conservative relative to the nominal Gaussian distribution. Consequently, we 
preserve the consistency of the pose and map estimates and avoid the ef f ects of an 
overconf i dent posterior. 
We demonstrate our i lter alongside the SEIF and the standard EKF both in sim- 
ulation as well as on two real-world datasets. While we maintain the computational 
advantages of an exactly sparse representation, the results show convincingly that 
our method yields conservative estimates for the robot pose and map that are nearly 
identical to those of the original Gaussian distribution as produced by the EKF, but 
at much less computational expense. 
The thesis concludes with an extension of our SLAM i lter to a complex underwater 
environment. We describe a systems-level framework for localization and mapping 
relative to a ship hull with an Autonomous Underwater Vehicle (AUV) equipped 
with a forward-looking sonar. The approach utilizes our i lter to fuse measurements 
of vehicle attitude and motion from onboard sensors with data from sonar images of 
the hull. We employ the system to perform three-dimensional, 6-DOF SLAM on a 
ship hull. 
Thesis Supervisor: John J. Leonard 
Title: Professor of Mechanical and Ocean Engineering 
Massachusetts Institute of Technology1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTMatthew R. Walter's PhD thesis, "Sparse Bayesian Information Filters for Localization and Mapping," presents a new estimation framework for Simultaneous Localization and Mapping (SLAM), aiming to address scalability issues in large environments. The thesis outlines an estimation-theoretic algorithm that significantly improves computational efficiency while maintaining consistent estimates for vehicle pose and environmental mapping.The work specifically deals with the feature-based SLAM problem, using a collection of landmarks to represent the environment. The thesis employs a Bayesian approach, maintaining a joint posterior over vehicle pose and feature states based on measurement data. The distribution is modeled as Gaussian and parametrized in the canonical form using an information (inverse covariance) matrix. This representation facilitates computationally efficient Bayesian SLAM filtering when sparse.An analysis of the Sparse Extended Information Filter (SEIF), an existing algorithm that approximates the sparsity of the information matrix, is also conducted in the thesis. Walter identifies that the SEIF results in an inconsistent, overconfident posterior approximation that negatively impacts the SLAM process and its overarching goal of accurate map production for future localization and path planning.In response, the thesis introduces a new scalable filter that maintains sparsity while preserving the consistency of the distribution. This algorithm utilizes the structure of the feature-based canonical parametrization to maintain an exactly sparse posterior, achieving efficiency gains with a computational cost that scales linearly with the map size.The proposed method is demonstrated alongside the SEIF and the standard Extended Kalman Filter (EKF) using simulation and two real-world datasets. Results show that the new method provides conservative estimates for robot pose and map nearly identical to those produced by the EKF, but at a significantly reduced computational expense.The thesis concludes with an application of the SLAM filter to an underwater environment, utilizing an Autonomous Underwater Vehicle (AUV) equipped with a forward-looking sonar for localization and mapping relative to a ship hull. This system is employed to perform three-dimensional, 6-DOF SLAM on a ship hull.1 / 1Statler: State-Maintaining Language Models 
for Embodied Reasoning 
Takuma Yonedaâ1, Jiading Fangâ1, Peng Liâ2, Huanyu Zhangâ3, Tianchong Jiang3, Shengjie Lin1, 
Ben Picker3, David Yunis1, Hongyuan Mei1, and Matthew R. Walter1 
1Toyota Technological Institute at Chicago 
{takuma,fjd,slin,dyunis,hongyuan,mwalter}@ttic.edu 
2Fudan University 
lip21@m.fudan.edu.cn 
3University of Chicago 
{huanyu,tianchongj,bpicker}@uchicago.edu 
Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) provide a promising tool that enable 
robots to perform complex robot reasoning tasks. However, the limited context 
window of contemporary LLMs makes reasoning over long time horizons diff i cult. 
Embodied tasks such as those that one might expect a household robot to perform 
typically require that the planner consider information acquired a long time ago 
(e.g., properties of the many objects that the robot previously encountered in the 
environment). Attempts to capture the world state using an LLMâs implicit internal 
representation is complicated by the paucity of task- and environment-relevant 
information available in a robotâs action history, while methods that rely on the 
ability to convey information via the prompt to the LLM are subject to its limited 
context window. In this paper, we propose Statler, a framework that endows LLMs 
with an explicit representation of the world state as a form of âmemoryâ that is 
maintained over time.Integral to Statler is its use of two instances of general 
LLMsâa world-model reader and a world-model writerâthat interface with and 
maintain the world state. By providing access to this world state âmemoryâ, Statler 
improves the ability of existing LLMs to reason over longer time horizons without 
the constraint of context length. We evaluate the effectiveness of our approach on 
three simulated table-top manipulation domains and a real robot domain, and show 
that it improves the state-of-the-art in LLM-based robot reasoning. Project website: 
https://statler-lm.github.io/. 
Keywords: Large language models, Long-horizon planning, World state model 
1Introduction 
Large language models (LLMs) are capable of generating intricate free-form text and complex 
code with an impressive level of prof i ciency [1,2,3]. Recently, researchers have shown that the 
success of LLMs extends to robotics domains, where the capacity for LLMs to perform complex 
reasoning using language enables robots to perform tasks that require sophisticated planning and 
language understanding [4,5,6]. These methods either rely solely on the implicit in-context memory 
that is internal to the LLM [5] or they augment LLMs with scene information extracted from an 
ego-centric image captured at the current time step [4]. Both approaches have proven effective 
for diff i cult embodied reasoning tasks, however they struggle when faced with planning tasks that 
require planning over long time horizons, due to the limited context window of contemporary LLMs. 
Although there have been recent efforts to enlarge the context window of LLMs [7], the size of the

Figure 1: Our Statler framework enables robots to carry out complex tasks specif i ed in natural 
language that require reasoning over long time horizons. Integral to our model are its world model 
writer and world model reader, two instances of general LLMs that are responsible for maintaining 
the explicit world state and generating code that enables the robot to carry out the task. 
context window remains fundamentally bounded. Further, providing the model with long-range 
context improves prediction accuracy only on on a small number of tokensâLLMs struggle to exploit 
information conveyed in long-term context beyond what can be directly copied [8]. Meanwhile, 
reliance on the robotâs current ego-centric view prohibits the language model from reasoning over 
aspects of the scene that are not directly observable, e.g., the fruit located in the (closed) kitchen 
refrigerator or an object in a room that the robot previously visited. 
In this paper, we propose Statler (STATe-maintaining Language models for Embodied Reasoning), 
a framework that maintains an external world model as explicit memory to improve the long-term 
reasoning capabilities of LLMs for robot planning. Integral to our approach, as shown in Figure 1, 
it maintains and interfaces with this world model over time using two instances of general LLMsâ 
a world-model reader and a world-model writer. The world-model reader interfaces with the 
world model to generate code that answers user queries. The world-model writer is responsible for 
predicting the next world state based on the current world state and a query given by the reader. 
We employ a structured representation of the world state, which has been found to improve the 
performance of LLMs [9,10], particularly when the output is also structured, and has the advantage 
of being human-readable and concise for eff i cient processing. Note that while we individually tailor 
each world modelâs design to its general task type (see Prompts 12, 8, 7, and 9), the design is highly 
l exible because the reader and writer are both LLMs and are instructed with in-context-learning 
to understand how to parse and manipulate the world model. This is in contrast to domain-specif i c 
formal languages [11], where the designs are i xed and parsing and writing requires that specif i c rules 
be followed. 
We evaluate Statler on a series of simulated and real-world robot manipulation domains. Experimental 
results demonstrate that Statler improves the long-term embodied reasoning capabilities of LLMs 
and that it outperforms the current state-of-the-art [5]. 1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPT"Statler: State-Maintaining Language Models for Embodied Reasoning" is a research paper by Takuma Yoneda, Jiading Fang, Peng Li, Huanyu Zhang, Tianchong Jiang, Shengjie Lin, Ben Picker, David Yunis, Hongyuan Mei, and Matthew R. Walter. It presents a novel framework designed to improve the long-term reasoning capabilities of large language models (LLMs) for robotic planning.LLMs have demonstrated success in generating intricate free-form text, complex code, and facilitating robot task performance that requires sophisticated planning and language understanding. However, contemporary LLMs have a limited context window, which makes reasoning over longer time horizons difficult, especially in embodied tasks that a household robot might be expected to perform.In response to this challenge, the authors propose Statler, a framework that supplies LLMs with an explicit, time-maintained representation of the world state, functioning as a form of "memory". Key to Statler are two instances of general LLMs, namely a world-model reader and a world-model writer. These models interface with and maintain the world state, enhancing the capability of LLMs to reason over longer time horizons without being constrained by context length.The world-model reader interacts with the world model to generate code that answers user queries. On the other hand, the world-model writer predicts the next world state based on the current world state and a query provided by the reader. The authors utilize a structured representation of the world state, which is beneficial in improving LLM performance, particularly when the output is also structured. Furthermore, it provides the advantages of being human-readable and concise for efficient processing.The effectiveness of Statler is evaluated on three simulated table-top manipulation domains and a real-world robot domain. The results demonstrate that Statler improves the long-term embodied reasoning capabilities of LLMs and outperforms the existing state-of-the-art approaches in LLM-based robot reasoning.1 / 12. Despite this progress, Word2Vec-type1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThe recent history of natural language processing (NLP) has been marked by significant evolution and advancement, driven by transformative models based on the Transformer architecture. These models can generate texts that are coherent enough to fool readers without relying on deductive and symbolic logic pre-set by a programmer. This method imitates some cognitive mechanisms of the human brain through artificial neurons - tiny computer programs that activate or deactivate themselves based on their calculation outcomes.This method gained prominence after Geoffrey Hinton's team won the 2012 ImageNet competition, demonstrating the effectiveness of using large amounts of computing power, a vast dataset, and a connectionist approach.The recent evolution of NLP is marked by four significant changes:The first change was the development of Word2vec, a group of language models that aim to reconstruct the linguistic context in which words are used. Word2vec models the information words contain using algorithms. It vectorizes words using two complementary architectures. CBOW (Continuous Bag of Words) predicts a word based on the five words to its right and left. Conversely, Skip-gram predicts the context words from a given word. However, Word2vec has limitations - it assigns only one meaning per word and only vectorizes individual words, which means it struggles to understand the meaning of even a simple sentence.To address these limitations, Vinyals and Le proposed applying a sequential approach to Word2Vec, which allows the model to consider a minimum of contextual information. Their method, outlined in the paper "A Neural Conversational Model", models the meaning of a text by linking certain sequences with others, effectively enabling a networked form of text mapping that includes larger sequences like sentences.1 / 1Despite this progress, Word2Vec-type systems are still based on the ap-

As explained in the paper "Deep contextualized word representations" (Peters et al., 2018), ELMo allows the model to recognize the dynamic or situational nature of word meaning. Each word is assigned a weight according to its influence in the sentence, allowing the model to distinguish the different meanings of a word like "bow" based on its context.ELMo introduced a novel way of modeling that reads the text in three different ways: from left to right, in reverse, and by combining the vectorized meanings of both types of analysis. It marked the real beginning of pre-trained models, which could save users from having to train their models from scratch on massive data sets, an expensive and time-consuming task.The fourth significant development was the introduction of the Transformer architecture, as outlined in the paper "Attention is all you need" (Vaswani et al., 2017). Transformer architecture improved on previous models by identifying the context that gives meaning to words and processing them in parallel using an encoder and a decoder. This approach helped solve problems related to memory, computational speed, and word position in previous models.The Transformer architecture's real innovation lies in its attention mechanism. It computes a weighted matrix product, effectively calculating a score that determines the level of attention a word should pay to other words. In other words, it computes situational dependency. An encoder can compute several "attention heads" that work bidirectionally, using computed attention weights as input to produce an output vector.These advancements all rely on deep learning, an approach made famous by Hinton's 2012 demonstration, and they leverage neural networks. These networks can have multiple layers of encoders and decoders, with the only limits being technical ones.1 / 1It is thus these new attention mechanisms specif i c to Transformer archi-

However, the 1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTAt the time of writing, GPT-3 is the most powerful language model, trained on around 570 gigabytes of data and composed of 175 billion parameters (Brown et al. 2020). GPT-3 is intended to generate text, favoring the decoder part of the Transformer architecture. Unlike BERT, GPT-3 is not open-source but marketed via an Application Programming Interface (API). It is a part of a strategic approach aiming to control the economic ecosystem of future companies. By March 2021, more than 300 companies were using this API, with its usage still growing.Despite the remarkable abilities of these models, they are far from perfect. A crucial challenge they face is dealing with the data they are trained on. Given that all language models are trained on existing text data, they are likely to absorb the biases present in that data. For example, GPT-3 was trained on data sourced from Reddit and Wikipedia, platforms that have been shown to have demographic skews. Consequently, the biases in the training data can lead to biased outputs when the models are used.This issue also becomes a problem on the output side, with multiple potential and actual concerns. One of them is 'astroturfing', where large numbers of automated responses are generated to simulate a mass movement in support of certain organizations or ideas. Another concern is the production of harmful or inappropriate content. For instance, GPT-3 was used in AI Dungeon, a game which generated narratives that involved inappropriate content, a consequence not foreseen by OpenAI.The critique of these language models was detailed in the paper "Stochastic Parrots", which highlighted three major issues related to the increasing size of these models: their environmental cost, their formal and rigid nature that allows biases to structure themselves and often go unnoticed, and potential solutions to mitigate the risks associated with their use.The paper argues that these models are trained only on the form of the language, not its substance. Thus, the models may seem to master language, but they only have a statistical understanding of it. The paper forms part of a broader critique of Natural Language Processing (NLP), which includes issues with biases in the training data and the inability of these models to truly comprehend language and context beyond a statistical level.1 / 1Open in app

GPT4 kept the model secret to avoid competition, now the secret is out!

The GPT4 model has been THE groundbreaking model so far, available to the general public either for free or through their commercial portal (for public beta use). It has worked wonders in igniting new project ideas and use-cases for many entrepreneurs but the secrecy about the number of parameters and the model was killing all enthusiasts who were betting on the first 1 trillion parameter model to 100 trillion parameter claims!

The cat is out of the bag
Well, the cat is out of the bag (Sort of). On June 20th, George Hotz, founder of self-driving startup Comma.ai leaked that GPT-4 isnât a single monolithic dense model (like GPT-3 and GPT-3.5) but a mixture of 8 x 220-billion-parameter models.


Later that day, Soumith Chintala, co-founder of PyTorch at Meta, reaffirmed the leak.


Just the day before, Mikhail Parakhin, Microsoft Bing AI lead, had also hinted at this.


GPT 4: Not a Monolith
What do all the tweets mean? The GPT-4 is not a single large model but a union/ensemble of 8 smaller models sharing the expertise. Each of these models is rumored to be 220 Billion parameters.


The methodology is called a mixture of experts' model paradigms (linked below). It's a well-known methodology also called as hydra of model. It reminds me of Indian mythology I will go with Ravana.


Please take it with a grain of salt that it is not official news but significantly high-ranking members in the AI community have spoken/hinted towards it. Microsoft is yet to confirm any of these.

What is a Mixture of Experts paradigm?
Now that we have spoken about the mixture of experts, let's take a little bit of a dive into what that thing is. The Mixture of Experts is an ensemble learning technique developed specifically for neural networks. It differs a bit from the general ensemble technique from the conventional machine learning modeling (that form is a generalized form). So you can consider that the Mixture of Experts in LLMs is a special case for ensemble methods.

In short, in this method, a task is divided into subtasks, and experts for each subtask are used to solve the models. It is a way to divide and conquer approach while creating decision trees. One could also consider it as meta-learning on top of the expert models for each separate task.

A smaller and better model can be trained for each sub-task or problem type. A meta-model learns to use which model is better at predicting a particular task. Meta learner/model acts as a traffic cop. The sub-tasks may or may not overlap, which means that a combination of the outputs can be merged together to come up with the final output.

For the concept-descriptions from MOE to Pooling, all credits to the great blog by Jason Brownlee (https://machinelearningmastery.com/mixture-of-experts/). If you like what you read below, please please subscribe to Jasonâs blog and buy a book or two to support his amazing work!

Mixture of experts, MoE or ME for short, is an ensemble learning technique that implements the idea of training experts on subtasks of a predictive modeling problem.

In the neural network community, several researchers have examined the decomposition methodology. [â¦] MixtureâofâExperts (ME) methodology that decomposes the input space, such that each expert examines a different part of the space. [â¦] A gating network is responsible for combining the various experts.

â Page 73, Pattern Classification Using Ensemble Methods, 2010.

There are four elements to the approach, they are:

Division of a task into subtasks.
Develop an expert for each subtask.
Use a gating model to decide which expert to use.
Pool predictions and gating model output to make a prediction.
The figure below, taken from Page 94 of the 2012 book âEnsemble Methods,â provides a helpful overview of the architectural elements of the method.


Example of a Mixture of Experts Model with Expert Members and a Gating Network
Taken from: Ensemble Methods
Subtasks
The first step is to divide the predictive modeling problem into subtasks. This often involves using domain knowledge. For example, an image could be divided into separate elements such as background, foreground, objects, colors, lines, and so on.

â¦ ME works in a divide-and-conquer strategy where a complex task is broken up into several simpler and smaller subtasks, and individual learners (called experts) are trained for different subtasks.

â Page 94, Ensemble Methods, 2012.

For those problems where the division of the task into subtasks is not obvious, a simpler and more generic approach could be used. For example, one could imagine an approach that divides the input feature space by groups of columns or separates examples in the feature space based on distance measures, inliers, and outliers for a standard distribution, and much more.

â¦ in ME, a key problem is how to find the natural division of the task and then derive the overall solution from sub-solutions.

â Page 94, Ensemble Methods, 2012.

Expert Models
Next, an expert is designed for each subtask.

The mixture of experts approach was initially developed and explored within the field of artificial neural networks, so traditionally, experts themselves are neural network models used to predict a numerical value in the case of regression or a class label in the case of classification.

It should be clear that we can âplug inâ any model for the expert. For example, we can use neural networks to represent both the gating functions and the experts. The result is known as a mixture density network.

â Page 344, Machine Learning: A Probabilistic Perspective, 2012.

Experts each receive the same input pattern (row) and make a prediction.

Gating Model
A model is used to interpret the predictions made by each expert and to aid in deciding which expert to trust for a given input. This is called the gating model, or the gating network, given that it is traditionally a neural network model.

The gating network takes as input the input pattern that was provided to the expert models and outputs the contribution that each expert should have in making a prediction for the input.

â¦ the weights determined by the gating network are dynamically assigned based on the given input, as the MoE effectively learns which portion of the feature space is learned by each ensemble member

â Page 16, Ensemble Machine Learning, 2012.

The gating network is key to the approach and effectively, the model learns to choose the type subtask for a given input and, in turn, the expert to trust to make a strong prediction.

Mixture-of-experts can also be seen as a classifier selection algorithm, where individual classifiers are trained to become experts in some portion of the feature space.

â Page 16, Ensemble Machine Learning, 2012.

When neural network models are used, the gating network and the experts are trained together such that the gating network learns when to trust each expert to make a prediction. This training procedure was traditionally implemented using expectation maximization (EM). The gating network might have a softmax output that gives a probability-like confidence score for each expert.

In general, the training procedure tries to achieve two goals: for given experts, to find the optimal gating function; for a given gating function, to train the experts on the distribution specified by the gating function.

â Page 95, Ensemble Methods, 2012.

Pooling Method
Finally, the mixture of expert models must make a prediction, and this is achieved using a pooling or aggregation mechanism. This might be as simple as selecting the expert with the largest output or confidence provided by the gating network.

Alternatively, a weighted sum prediction could be made that explicitly combines the predictions made by each expert and the confidence estimated by the gating network. You might imagine other approaches to making effective use of the predictions and gating network output.

The pooling/combining system may then choose a single classifier with the highest weight, or calculate a weighted sum of the classifier outputs for each class, and pick the class that receives the highest weighted sum.

â Page 16, Ensemble Machine Learning, 2012.

Switch Routing
We should also briefly discuss the switch routing approach differs from the MoE paper. I am bringing it up as it seems like Microsoft has used a switch routing than a Model of Experts to save some computational complexity, but I am happy to be proven wrong. When there are more than one expert's models, they may have a non-trivial gradient for the routing function (which model to use when). This decision boundary is controlled by the switch layer.

The benefits of the switch layer are threefold.

Routing computation is reduced if the token is being routed only to a single expert model
The batch size (expert capacity) can be at least halved since a single token goes to a single model
The routing implementation is simplified and communications are reduced.
The overlap of the same token to more than 1 expert model is called as the Capacity factor. Following is a conceptual depiction of how routing with different expert capacity factors works


illustration of token routing dynamics. Each expert processes a fixed batch-size
of tokens modulated by the capacity factor. Each token is routed to the expert
with the highest router probability, but each expert has a fixed batch size of
(total tokens/num experts) Ã capacity factor. If the tokens are unevenly dis-
patched, then certain experts will overflow (denoted by dotted red lines), resulting
in these tokens not being processed by this layer. A larger capacity factor allevi-
ates this overflow issue but also increases computation and communication costs
(depicted by padded white/empty slots). (source https://arxiv.org/pdf/2101.03961.pdf)
When compared with the MoE, findings from the MoE and Switch paper suggest that

Switch transformers outperform carefully tuned dense models and MoE transformers on a speed-quality basis.
Switch transformers have a smaller compute futprint than MoE
Switch transformers perform better at lower capacity factors (1â1.25).

Concluding thoughts
Two caveats, first, that this is all coming from hearsay, and second, my understanding of these concepts is fairly feeble, so I urge readers to take it with a boulder of salt.

But what did Microsoft achieve by keeping this architecture hidden? Well, they created a buzz, and suspense around it. This might have helped them to craft their narratives better. They kept innovation to themselves and avoided others catching up to them sooner. The whole idea was likely a usual Microsoft gameplan of thwarting competition while they invest 10B into a company.

GPT-4 performance is great, but it was not an innovative or breakthrough design. It was an amazingly clever implementation of the methods developed by engineers and researchers topped up by an enterprise/capitalist deployment. OpenAI has neither denied or agreed to these claims (https://thealgorithmicbridge.substack.com/p/gpt-4s-secret-has-been-revealed), which makes me think that this architecture for GPT-4 is more than likely the reality (which is great!). Just not cool! We all want to know and learn.

1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThis article discusses the architecture of OpenAI's GPT-4 language model. In it, the author reveals that GPT-4 is not a monolithic dense model like its predecessors (GPT-3 and GPT-3.5), but instead consists of an ensemble of eight smaller models. Each of these models is purported to contain 220 billion parameters.This type of design is known as a "mixture of experts" (MoE) model paradigm. It's a well-established methodology in which tasks are divided into subtasks and then assigned to "experts" for solving. The individual models or "experts" can be trained on smaller, more manageable problem types. A meta-model, referred to as the "gating model," learns when to use which model to predict a specific task, effectively acting as a traffic cop.The article further explains the concept of switch routing, which could be used instead of a MoE model to save computational complexity. It differs from the MoE model in that it simplifies the routing implementation, reduces communication, and requires a smaller batch size since a single token goes to a single model. According to recent research, Switch transformers outperform MoE transformers on a speed-quality basis and have a smaller computational footprint.In conclusion, the author stresses that although GPT-4's performance is impressive, the model's architecture was not an innovative or groundbreaking design. Instead, it was an ingenious application of existing methods by engineers and researchers. The author also expresses some frustration with OpenAI's lack of transparency regarding GPT-4's design, asserting that the decision to keep the architecture secret was likely driven by a desire to stave off competition.1 / 1How is this related to active inference and Reinterpreting Free-Energy Principle?1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThe Free Energy Principle (FEP) is a theoretical framework developed by Karl Friston that attempts to explain how intelligent systems, such as the human brain, maintain their existence and adapt to their environment. It is a unifying theory for understanding cognition, action, and perception in biological systems, based on the minimization of free energy, which is a concept borrowed from thermodynamics and statistical physics.Active Inference, on the other hand, is a process theory derived from the Free Energy Principle. It offers a comprehensive framework that treats perception and action as two sides of the same coin. In simple terms, active inference suggests that organisms act in ways that minimize the difference between their expectations and their sensory inputs. They are continuously updating their beliefs about the world and acting to minimize the surprise associated with these sensory inputs.In the context of AI models such as GPT-4, the connection to these principles is not straightforward because these AI models are not inherently designed to minimize free energy or perform active inference. However, the notion of using multiple expert models within GPT-4 to address different parts of the problem space could be viewed as an indirect relation to these principles.The ensemble of expert models in GPT-4 can be seen as analogous to an organism's different cognitive systems or subsystems, each specialized for different tasks and constantly updating their beliefs based on new data. This could be interpreted as an embodiment of the Free Energy Principle and active inference, where each sub-model within the ensemble acts to minimize its prediction error (analogous to free energy) for its specific task.However, it's important to note that this is an analogy or reinterpretation. In the strict sense, these AI models are not based on the Free Energy Principle or active inference. Their design and optimization are driven by the specific tasks they are trained for, typically through methods like gradient descent on a defined loss function, which isn't directly related to the ideas of free energy or active inference.Lastly, there are ongoing research efforts to incorporate ideas from the Free Energy Principle and active inference into AI and machine learning models, but this is a complex, challenging area that is still in the early stages of exploration as of my knowledge cutoff in September 2021.1 / 1AIâ-âThe Maker vs. The Operator, Comparing LLM vs. Active Inference AI

âAI that grows in knowledge, perception and awareness through the evolving real-time data produced by all things, at all times, throughout our living, breathing, and ever-changing world."

This is Active Inference AI, an entirely new type of networked AI that is designed for Real-Time Operations, including things like managing the daily operations of a hospital, an airport, global supply chains, an entire smart city, global drone and autonomous vehicle traffic, and other real-world activities. This introduces a new era of artificial intelligence which enables us to streamline, optimize and automate every mission-critical, real-life function that we interact with every day.

The days of training AI on big data with probabilistic results will give way to an interconnected âInternet of Everythingâ that deploys Active Inference AI throughout the network; inherently secure and accurate because it takes any point of real-time data and makes it smart through an empowered ecosystem of interconnected AI Apps called Intelligent Agents. This networked system operates in tandem with humans, growing its intelligence in sync with humans and the world.

What makes a predictive machine learning model different from the Active Inference AI of the Spatial Web?
Deep Fakes of Intelligence â stochastic parrots.

A Machine Model displays superficial intelligence. It can only give answers with a guess that is accurate to the point of the answer having been included in its training data, otherwise it will make up an answer. It is all based on probability of accuracy, but never 100%. And even at that, it can still be wrong with a tendency to make things up, attempting to make it sound appropriate.

An appropriate output is very different from an accurate output.

Active Inference AI with the Spatial Web Protocol is accurate because itâs based on real-time, real life data that is continually updating as the changing details and context around any entity in any space unfolds over time.

Predictive machines are great as content generators, idea enhancers, and task execution. Active inference can make decisions incorporating real-time data. Active Inference can run the planet. These are two very different animals.

Generative and Transformer AI models have no ability for comprehension, awareness, or perception. They possess no capacity to reason or plan. They have no understanding of how the world works. Predictive pattern matching machines can fake it, given enough data that it models from, but they are merely interpolating and regurgitating the patterns they have recognized that are already in use and familiar to us.

A GPT will tell you a great story about your city, but it canât control the traffic within your city in real-time because it does not look outward at the world around us. Instead, it looks inward to its historical memory which consists of billions of parameters and words from the past that were downloaded into it. If it tells you a story about New York, that narrative is based on the words and references that it has been exposed to, not what is happening in New York right this instant. And because it has no window into the actual real-time activities within New York itself, it can make tremendous errors which would be unacceptable for mission-critical applications like Smart Cities, or Banking, or Policing, or the myriad other real-world applications in which we need AI1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThe author, Denise Holt, offers a compelling comparison of traditional machine learning models, like GPT-4 (referred to as "LLM" or "Language Learning Models" in the text), and the newer Active Inference AI systems developed by VERSES AI. The comparison primarily revolves around two points:The Source of Intelligence and Real-time Response: Traditional models like GPT-4 are trained on vast amounts of data and learn to predict or generate outputs based on patterns and structures in this data. However, they are not designed to interact with or adapt to the real-time state of the world. On the other hand, Active Inference AI systems are designed to continuously ingest real-time data and adapt their responses accordingly. They are "aware" of the changing state of the world, enabling them to make decisions and take actions based on the most up-to-date information.The Nature and Scope of Tasks: GPT-4 and similar models excel at tasks like content generation and enhancement, which involve creating or modifying textual data. But they are not equipped to control real-world systems or processes. Active Inference AI systems are designed to manage real-world operations, like controlling traffic in a city or managing the logistics of a global supply chain. They are capable of making real-time decisions based on real-time data, which is crucial for these kinds of tasks.The piece also explores the idea of a global "Internet of Everything" that is empowered by Active Inference AI. In this vision, every piece of data is continuously updated and made "smart" by a network of AI apps called Intelligent Agents. These agents can operate in collaboration with human users, growing in intelligence as they interact with the world and each other.As Holt points out, the concept of Active Inference AI takes us beyond the realm of AI as a content generator or task executor. Instead, it positions AI as a real-time decision maker and manager, capable of running complex systems and processes in concert with human operators. This represents a significant step forward in the potential applications and impact of AI technology.However, it's important to note that while the potential of Active Inference AI is significant, the practical implementation and deployment of such systems will involve significant technical and logistical challenges. It's an exciting area of research and development, but there is still much work to be done to realize the full potential of this technology.1 / 1Fluent vs. Factual
LLMs are well-versed because the statistics of fluency are easily obtained from the training data. Logic and accuracy are a much harder ask.

GPT-3, ChatGPT, LLaMA, Bing Chat, and GPT-4 are all autoregressive LLMs. Autoregressive generative model structure operates in a way that when structuring a language sequence, it produces one token after another, reflecting on the result of the previous token to determine what to produce next. (A token is a word or sub word.)

At each step of token production, there is a point of probability taking place for steering toward accuracy or inaccuracy. There will always be probability that the next token produced can take the model down an incorrect path.

Each token produced provides an opportunity to branch off into a unique response, while also producing a new opportunity to stray from the truth. This deviation plays out exponentially with each new fork that is taking cues from the previous token, analyzing it to determine its next move. Each prediction impacts every prediction thereafter. Without reasoning and understanding, these errors are likely to compound as the process feeds on those errors.

At no point is the machine able to ask itself if this string of text entries makes intellectual and reasonable sense.

This problem cannot be fixed with LLMs. There will always be an element of possibility for the model results to go haywire. This is where the AI hallucination factor comes in.

A model based on probability is unmanageable in a continuous environment because if/when it goes off track, thereâs no way to rein it back in.

For AI to become factual, it would have to accurately predict every detail of the world.

Enter the Long-Awaited World Model
Spatial Web Protocol HSTP and HSML for the World Model and model predictive control.

VERSES AI is expanding the internet for networked intelligence.

There is a fundamental power within a connected world: collective intelligence, a hive mind.

This computable and distributed intelligence is what powers the AI of the future.

By building a new protocol as a public standard, and then giving it away free to the public, VERSES AI has enabled a frictionless network for scalability.

HSML is the programming language that computes context, enabling the AIâs perception to understand the real-time changing state of anything in the world.

HSTP, the Spatial Web Protocol, will recognize any person place or thing as a unique entity within this network, providing it with a unique ID, and acting as a gatekeeper for access, interaction, and transaction by and between all things.

Any thing inside of any space becomes a nested domain uniquely identifiable and programmable within a digital twin of the earth, producing a model for data normalization.

Contingencies and changing details and circumstances for all objects and situations can now be measured and computed, providing a basis for AI perception, affecting all entities and their interrelationships to each other.

Connecting the planet is a massive undertaking that can only be enacted by putting it in the hands of individuals, developers, and builders. For AI, this is the critical piece for scaling AI to AGI.

This is what VERSE AI has built.

So What Makes Active Inference So Accurate?
Active Inference AI is based on biological design â Embodied AI â with the ability to take action. This the core engine.

Active Inference is so accurate because it continually looks outward into the world, measuring the world, in real-time through a global network of sensors â IoT devices, cameras, robots, drones â anything that is connected within the Spatial Web â the Digital Twin network of the world. This mimics the way humans and animals make decisions. As we use our senses to SEE the world âas it isâ â moment by moment â we can then more accurately estimate what might happen if we perform an action.

As a human, if we are walking down the street and see a reckless driver coming toward us, we recognize it, notice the erratic actions, infer what would happen if we stood in the way of the car, and we can make the decision to jump out of the way, and save ourselves. We do this because we are able to take in all of the information about the situation and the context involved. The car, the driver, the speed, the pattern of driving, where we stand in relation to the trajectory of the moving vehicle. Through our senses, we obtain awareness to the situation, and therefore can make decisions based on the perception feedback loop that is updating moment by moment as the car gets closer â to the point of understanding exactly how to take action â when to jump and in which direction to go.

In the Spatial Web, Active Inference AI can reach out and interact with the world through all the networked IoT sensors, machines, and the context of HSML informing the constantly changing details and characteristics of the interrelationships between all things. This creates a cybernetic feedback loop of Perception of the World, updating its model of the world, with belief of what it knows to be true; gaining understanding of the intricacies and inner workings of world so that it can make decisions, and take Action. The more this feedback loop plays out, the AI learns more about the world and the results of actions taken, (just as a child learns about its world as it grows and interacts with it), and the more accurate this AI becomes by further updating its understanding of the World.

Intelligent Agents with Agency
AIs vs. IAs

AIs are large, siloed machines with independent functions. IAs are intelligent agents with agency for interoperable behavior.

The Spatial Web creates an ecosystem of distributed intelligence with computational context equipping Intelligent Agents with the right kind of data: data that specifies the details, characteristics, and attributes about any thing in any space and how they interact and relate to each other.

This enables the Intelligent Agents to have agency to function through self-organization using belief updating (what it knows to be true at any given moment). It is a system of distributed cognition throughout a unified network containing multiple entities interacting intelligently with each other, communicating with each other, and sharing what they know to be true. This sort of data facilitated agency empowers these IAs to develop perception, awareness, and curiosity about their environment â the data surrounding all agents within the environment.

This enables curiosity about me and you.

According to VERSES AI Chief Scientist, Dr. Karl Friston, curiosity when referring to the Free Energy Principle is described as the resolution of uncertainty.

Dr. Friston describes curiosity this way, âto be curious you have to imagine what would happen if I did that, and what would I know if I did that, but youâll have to imagine it before itâs actually happened, which is the big bright line between the anthropomorphic kind of intelligence and the intelligence you find in the thermostat.â

A New Era of Networked Intelligence
VERSES AI has laid the foundation for a universal mind that is grounded in reality, as a digital twin of earth and anyything within it.

Active Inference is more like a biological organism that perceives and acts on our World by generating more accurate models, understandings, and beliefs about our World.

These ever more accurate World Models enable better decisions â a Smarter World. This is the true measure of intelligence.

We are used to thinking of web domain names as permanent addreses on the internet. 
We have these addresses to locate devices on the Internet, to navigate between pages on the web, or buildings via maps. At most, these require two dimensions. We exist and operate in the third dimension. We need digital addresses that can do the same.

Addresses
The space around us doesnât have any universally acceptable and accessible âaddress.â Whether physical postal addresses or the digital addresses that allow us to email and navigate between websites, none of these have a meaningful relationship to each other, and so there is no method for integrating the physical and digital worlds.

Domains
A Domain Name is an identifier in the form of an alpha-numerical phrase that represents an address. A Spatial Domain, however, points to a 3D volumetric spatial address made of coordinates that can be registered across Distributed Ledgers.

Spatial Domain Registry
Similar to ICANN which manages the registration activities for web domain names, a Spatial Domain Registry enables people and organizations to register Spatial Domain names and have them validated. To accommodate duplicate names in the physical world, the Spatial Domain registry will also contain its physical address, including country, state, and city location and other profile information.

Hyperspatial Domains and the "real" physical world
A Spatial Webâ¢ domain is a titled or credentialed n-dimensional space or location that confer the right to manage the policies of that Space. Spatial Domains are nested and must adhere to the laws of parent domains (Home > City > State > Federal) unless there are provisions to override them (e.g. US State Sovereignty). Spatial Domain Names can be real places (like âJoeâs Cafeâ, âRoman Colosseumâ) or virtual (like âThe Oasisâ or âHogwartsâ).1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThe concept of "Spatial Domains" that you've introduced is a step toward integrating the physical and digital worlds by assigning three-dimensional addresses to both real and virtual spaces. This advancement goes beyond the two-dimensional addressing systems we use for locating devices on the Internet or navigating between web pages and locations on a map.Spatial Domains provide a solution to the lack of universally acceptable and accessible addressing for the space around us. Regular domain names, whether they are for postal or digital purposes, do not have a meaningful relationship with each other, thus hindering the integration of the physical and digital worlds.A Spatial Domain, on the other hand, is a three-dimensional volumetric spatial address comprised of coordinates. These can be registered across Distributed Ledgers, similar to how traditional web domain names are registered. This registration and validation process is managed by a Spatial Domain Registry, akin to how ICANN manages the registration of web domain names.To ensure uniqueness and avoid confusion due to duplicate names, Spatial Domains also carry their physical address information, including country, state, and city location, along with other profile data.A unique feature of Spatial Domains is that they can represent both real places (e.g., "Joe's Cafe" or "Roman Colosseum") and virtual locations (e.g., "The Oasis" or "Hogwarts"). They are also nested, meaning a smaller domain exists within a larger one, and must follow the rules of the parent domain, unless provisions allow for overriding them.In essence, Spatial Domains are a means to merge our physical reality with the digital realm, allowing for more accurate, meaningful, and efficient navigation and interaction in both.1 / 1Summarize: The Emperor's New Markov Blankets.

Abstract:
The free energy principle, an influential framework in computational neuroscience and theoretical neurobiology, starts from the assumption that living systems ensure adaptive exchanges with their environment by minimizing the objective function of variational free energy. Following this premise, it claims to deliver a promising integration of the life sciences. In recent work, Markov blankets, one of the central constructs of the free energy principle, have been applied to resolve debates central to philosophy (such as demarcating the boundaries of the mind). The aim of this paper is twofold. First, we trace the development of Markov blankets starting from their standard application in Bayesian networks, via variational inference, to their use in the literature on active inference. We then identify a persistent confusion in the literature between the formal use of Markov blankets as an epistemic tool for Bayesian inference, and their novel metaphysical use in the free energy framework to demarcate the physical boundary between an agent and its environment. Consequently, we propose to distinguish between "Pearl blankets" to refer to the original epistemic use of Markov blankets and "Friston blankets" to refer to the new metaphysical construct. Second, we use this distinction to critically assess claims resting on the application of Markov blankets to philosophical problems. We suggest that this literature would do well in differentiating between two different research programmes: "inference with a model" and "inference within a model." Only the latter is capable of doing metaphysical work with Markov blankets, but requires additional philosophical premises and cannot be justified by an appeal to the success of the mathematical framework alone.1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPT"The Emperor's New Markov Blankets" is an academic article that discusses the Free Energy Principle (FEP) and the use of Markov blankets, a concept integral to the FEP, in computational neuroscience and theoretical neurobiology. The FEP assumes that living systems maintain adaptive interactions with their environment by minimizing variational free energy, a specific mathematical function.The authors identify a common confusion in the scientific literature: the difference between the original use of Markov blankets for epistemic purposes in Bayesian inference (referred to as "Pearl blankets"), and their new application in the FEP to delineate the physical boundary between an agent and its environment ("Friston blankets").The article aims to achieve two main objectives:Track the evolution of Markov blankets from their standard application in Bayesian networks, via variational inference, to their usage in the active inference literature. The authors identify the recurrent confusion between the epistemic and metaphysical use of Markov blankets and propose the terminology of "Pearl blankets" and "Friston blankets" to distinguish between the two.Use the distinction between Pearl and Friston blankets to critically evaluate claims based on the application of Markov blankets to philosophical problems. They argue that understanding this distinction helps to differentiate between two different research programs: "inference with a model" and "inference within a model." Abstract:
This paper considers the relationship between thermodynamics, information and inference. In particular, it explores the thermodynamic concomitants of belief updating, under a variational (free energy) principle for self-organization. In brief, any (weakly mixing) random dynamical system that possesses a Markov blanket--i.e. a separation of internal and external states--is equipped with an information geometry. This means that internal states parametrize a probability density over external states. Furthermore, at non-equilibrium steady-state, the flow of internal states can be construed as a gradient flow on a quantity known in statistics as Bayesian model evidence. In short, there is a natural Bayesian mechanics for any system that possesses a Markov blanket. Crucially, this means that there is an explicit link between the inference performed by internal states and their energetics--as characterized by their stochastic thermodynamics.1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPT"Markov blankets, information geometry and stochastic thermodynamics" is an article written by Thomas Parr, Lancelot Da Costa, and Karl Friston. The paper explores the relationship between thermodynamics, information, and inference. In particular, it investigates the thermodynamic concomitants of belief updating, based on a variational (free energy) principle for self-organization. The authors note that any random dynamical system that possesses a Markov blanket - a separation of internal and external states - has an inherent information geometry. This means that the internal states can define a probability density over external states. The authors argue that there's a clear link between the inference performed by internal states and their energetics, as characterized by their stochastic thermodynamics, which contributes to our understanding of the Bayesian mechanics of systems with a Markov blanket.1 / 1Markov blankets and the preformationist assumption.

Abstract:
We show how a generic feature-selection algorithm returning strongly relevant variables can be turned into a causal structure-learning algorithm. We prove this under the Faithfulness assumption for the data distribution. In a causal graph, the strongly relevant variables for a node X are its parents, children, and children's parents (or spouses), also known as the Markov blanket of X. Identifying the spouses leads to the detection of the V-structure patterns and thus to causal orientations. Repeating the task for all variables yields a valid partially oriented causal graph. We first show an efficient way to identify the spouse links. We then perform several experiments in the continuous domain using the Recursive Feature Elimination feature-selection algorithm with Support Vector Regression and empirically verify the intuition of this direct (but computationally expensive) approach. Within the same framework, we then devise a fast and consistent algorithm, Total Conditioning (TC), and a variant, TCbw, with an explicit backward feature-selection heuristics, for Gaussian data. After running a series of comparative experiments on five artificial networks, we argue that Markov blanket algorithms such as TC/TCbw or Grow-Shrink scale better than the reference PC algorithm and provides higher structural accuracy.1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThe article "Using Markov Blankets for Causal Structure Learning" by Jean-Philippe Pellet and AndrÃ© Elisseeff explores how a feature-selection algorithm can be converted into a causal structure-learning algorithm. This is demonstrated under the Faithfulness assumption for the data distribution.In a causal graph, the variables that are strongly relevant for a node X include its parents, children, and children's parents (or spouses), also known as the Markov blanket of X. Identifying the spouses can lead to the detection of V-structure patterns, and thus to causal orientations. Applying this task to all variables results in a partially oriented causal graph.The authors propose an efficient way to identify spouse links and also present a new, fast, and consistent algorithm, Total Conditioning (TC), along with a variant, TCbw, which features an explicit backward feature-selection heuristics for Gaussian data.After conducting a series of comparative experiments on five artificial networks, the authors argue that Markov blanket algorithms like TC/TCbw or Grow-Shrink scale better than the reference PC algorithm and offer higher structural accuracy.1 / 1On Markov blankets and hierarchical self-organisation.

Abstract:
â¢ Computational treatment of biological self-organisation. â¢ Biological self-organisation requires emergence of boundaries, namely Markov blankets. â¢ Hierarchical self-organisation entails emergence of Markov blankets at multiple scale. Biological self-organisation can be regarded as a process of spontaneous pattern formation; namely, the emergence of structures that distinguish themselves from their environment. This process can occur at nested spatial scales: from the microscopic (e.g., the emergence of cells) to the macroscopic (e.g. the emergence of organisms). In this paper, we pursue the idea that Markov blankets â that separate the internal states of a structure from external states â can self-assemble at successively higher levels of organisation. Using simulations, based on the principle of variational free energy minimisation, we show that hierarchical self-organisation emerges when the microscopic elements of an ensemble have prior (e.g., genetic) beliefs that they participate in a macroscopic Markov blanket: i.e., they can only influence â or be influenced by â a subset of other elements. Furthermore, the emergent structures look very much like those found in nature (e.g., cells or organelles), when influences are mediated by short range signalling. These simulations are offered as a proof of concept that hierarchical self-organisation of Markov blankets (into Markov blankets) can explain the self-evidencing, autopoietic behaviour of biological systems.1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThe article "On Markov blankets and hierarchical self-organisation" by Ensor Rafael Palacios, Adeel Razi, Thomas Parr, Michael Kirchhoff, and Karl Friston discusses computational treatment of biological self-organisation.The authors assert that biological self-organisation can be understood as a process of spontaneous pattern formation, leading to the emergence of structures that distinguish themselves from their environment. However, it only goes into detail about two of them.The first major change is the development of Word2Vec models, as discussed in the paper "Efficient Estimation of Word Representations in Vector Space" by Mikolov et al. in 2013. This model, developed by a team from Google, aims to recreate the linguistic context in which words are used. Word2Vec employs a regression analysis method to relate the "meaning" of a word with its surrounding words. It involves turning words into vectors, meaning it represents the information contained in a word using algorithms. Word2Vec uses two architectures: CBOW (Continuous Bag of Words), which predicts a word based on the five words to its left and right, and Skip-gram, which predicts the surrounding context given a specific word. However, the model has limitations. It assigns only one meaning per word and only vectorizes individual words, which means it can't fully understand the meaning of even a simple sentence.The second major change addressed these limitations. Vinyals and Le, also from Google, proposed a sequential approach to Word2Vec in their paper "A Neural Conversational Model" in 2015. This method models the meaning of a text by connecting certain sequences, enabling modeling of larger sequences, including sentences. This approach takes into account the sentences preceding and following the target sentence, allowing for a minimum of contextual consideration.The passage hints at two more significant changes in NLP but doesn't go into detail. ELMo overcomes the limitation of Word2Vec and its ilk, where a word can only have one meaning. ELMo acknowledges the dynamic nature of word meanings, which can change depending on the context. Every word in a sentence is assigned a coefficient or "weight" based on its influence in that sentence. ELMo also introduced a method of analyzing the text in three ways: from left to right (beginning to end), in reverse (from end to beginning), and by combining the vectorized meanings of both types of analysis. It marked the real advent of pre-trained models, saving users from having to train their models from scratch on vast datasets.The fourth notable change came with the introduction of the Transformer architecture, as discussed in the paper "Attention is all you need" by Vaswani et al. in 2017. Transformer models addressed the limitations of previous sequential models, which had difficulties retaining information about the prioritization of terms among themselves, particularly in longer sequences. Transformers step away from this approach and resolve many memory, computational speed, and word position issues by identifying the context that gives meaning to words, which are then processed in parallel. They utilize an encoder, which turns information into code by assigning a calculated value to a word, and a decoder, which transforms code into information by "calculating" a word from a value. These models often employ multiple levels of encoders and decoders that act and provide feedback in a cybernetic manner.These four developments represent key milestones in the evolution of NLP, progressively improving the ability of algorithms to understand and generate human-like text.1 / 1h epointtokeepinmindhereisthatsucharchitecturesarebasedonneu- 
ral networks and on the âdeep learningâ made famous since Hintonâs demon- 
strationof2012,andinwhichlayersandlayersofencodersandlayersandlay- 
ers of decoders can be arranged without too many limits other than techni- 
calones.Aboveall,thetrulyinnovativecharacteroftheTransformerarchitec-

This attention mechanism calculates a "weighted matrix product" which determines the level of attention that a word should give to other words in the sentence, also understood as a situational dependency. The Transformers compute several "attention heads" that work bidirectionally, allowing for parallel processing of information by different attention heads, hence considerably reducing training times for language models compared to sequential models like Word2Vec.The use of these new attention mechanisms specific to Transformer architectures is the foundation for the recent successes of language models, including:Google's BERT (Bidirectional Encoder Representations from Transformers) was pre-trained on about 3.3 billion words and has 345 million parameters. BERT's primary objective is to end formalized keyword searches and enable users to express themselves in the most natural, user-friendly, and dialogical way possible. BERT focuses on the encoder part of the architecture, which transforms information into code. The Transformer architecture of BERT is highly flexible and acts as an interface between the natural input language and the output. Importantly, BERT is open-source, which is part of Google's specific corporate strategy of value creation.OpenAI's GPT-3 (Generative Pretrained Transformer 3) is one of the most powerful language models as of the time of writing, trained on around 570 gigabytes of data and composed of 175 billion parameters. Unlike BERT, GPT-3 aims to generate text and emphasizes the decoder part of its architecture, which transforms code into information. Unlike BERT, GPT-3 is not open-source. Instead, it's marketed via its Application Programming Interface (API), aligning with a corporate strategy to control the economic ecosystem on which many future companies will be based. As of March 2021, over 300 companies were reportedly using this API.These advancements have significantly shaped the current state of NLP, allowing for more nuanced understanding and generation of human-like text.1 / 1That was true at the time of writing however since the paper, gpt4 has been released: This article discusses the architecture of OpenAI's GPT-4 language model. In it, the author reveals that GPT-4 is not a monolithic dense model like its predecessors (GPT-3 and GPT-3.5), but instead consists of an ensemble of eight smaller models. Each of these models is purported to contain 220 billion parameters.

This type of design is known as a "mixture of experts" (MoE) model paradigm. It's a well-established methodology in which tasks are divided into subtasks and then assigned to "experts" for solving. The individual models or "experts" can be trained on smaller, more manageable problem types. A meta-model, referred to as the "gating model," learns when to use which model to predict a specific task, effectively acting as a traffic cop.1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThe GPT-4 language model developed by OpenAI introduces a significant change in the architecture of large language models. This new model operates based on a "mixture of experts" (MoE) paradigm, which contrasts with the monolithic dense model structure used in prior iterations such as GPT-3 and GPT-3.5.Here are the main points about GPT-4:GPT-4 is not a singular, dense model but comprises an ensemble of eight smaller models. Each of these models reportedly has 220 billion parameters.The design of GPT-4 is based on the "mixture of experts" (MoE) model paradigm. This methodology involves dividing tasks into subtasks and assigning them to specialized "experts" to solve.The individual models or "experts" in the GPT-4 structure can focus on smaller, more specific problem types, making the overall problem more manageable.GPT-4 introduces a "gating model," a meta-model that learns which model to use for predicting specific tasks, essentially serving as a controller or traffic cop.The shift to an MoE paradigm in GPT-4 demonstrates an innovative approach to handling complex tasks by dividing them among multiple specialized models. This new structure allows for more efficient processing and may provide improved performance in certain applications of language modeling.1 / 1Connections to the discussion so far1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThroughout this discussion, we've covered a range of topics relating to the evolution of natural language processing, from the advent of Word2Vec to Transformer architectures and the rise of BERT and GPT-3. Below are the main points connected to our discussion:Socially Constructed Training Data: Models like GPT and BERT rely on data that is itself a product of human social constructs. This means that the biases and limitations inherent in these sources inevitably get passed on to the AI models. For instance, the demographic disparities in the user bases of Reddit and Wikipedia are cited as a source of bias in GPT-2's training data.GIGO (Garbage In, Garbage Out) Principle: Because these models learn from the data they are trained on, any inaccuracies or biases in the training data will be reflected in the output. Hence, the principle of "garbage in, garbage out" applies. The input problems become output problems.Over-representation of Certain Constructs: Due to the statistical nature of these models, they tend to favor the most commonly occurring patterns in their training data. This means they could perpetuate stereotypes and other biases present in the data.Misinformation and Manipulation: The technology's ability to generate human-like text has implications for misinformation and manipulation. AI-powered propaganda is identified as a significant concern for the future.Unforeseen Consequences: The probabilistic nature of these models can lead to unintended outcomes. An example is the AI Dungeon controversy, where GPT-3 was found to be generating inappropriate content involving minors.Critique of NLP: The critique of NLP models is part of a broader critique of how these algorithms can reinforce existing societal biases. The "Stochastic Parrots" paper, which led to Timnit Gebru's dismissal from Google, highlights these issues.Understanding Language vs. Mimicking Language: The paper also highlights that these models, while appearing to understand language, only ever gain a statistical understanding of it. They can mimic the form of language (the signifier) but do not comprehend its substance (the signified).These concerns underscore the importance of responsible and ethical AI development and deployment. It's critical to consider these points while developing new iterations of AI models, like the shift from GPT-3 to GPT-4. The use of the "mixture of experts" model paradigm in GPT-4 could potentially help manage these issues more effectively, but the underlying challenges related to data biases and responsible AI usage remain.1 / 13. Here are the key insights related to our previous discussion:Hermeneutic Claims of AI: The text acknowledges the widespread belief in the ability of AI to comprehend and reason about language, often represented by statements from key figures in AI research and digital business. For example, it cites Facebook's claim about their platform being a "content understanding engine."Criticisms of AI Understanding: Criticisms often point out that AI understanding is superficial and more akin to the phenomenon of Clever Hans, a horse believed to solve arithmetic problems but in reality was merely responding to cues from its handler. These criticisms emphasize that AI models, like GPT-3 and BERT, lack a deep understanding of language and simply respond to the input patterns they've been trained on.Limits of 'Deep' Learning: Gary Marcus argues that the depth in 'deep learning' is primarily architectural and technical, rather than symbolic or hermeneutic. He identifies three key limitations:Lack of Compositionality: Models lack the ability to deal with complex meanings and narratives, a process akin to the hermeneutic circle where the whole and parts of a text inform each other.Absence of Background Knowledge: These models don't incorporate background or contextual knowledge into their understanding. They operate with their internal tools like probability, distance, variation, or threshold, calculating meanings but not considering their broader cultural or historical contexts.Semantic Issues: While AI can generate syntactically correct sentences, it lacks a meaningful understanding of the semantics of those sentences, with no real-world context or grounding.These insights remind us that while AI models like GPT-4 may seem to understand and generate human-like text, they lack genuine comprehension and contextual understanding. These models can mimic the form of language but do not grasp its underlying substance or meaning. Yes, the story of Akhfash's goat can be seen as a representation of several different themes and ideas.
